<p><b>Old Norse</b> was a <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">North Germanic language</a> that was spoken by inhabitants of <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> and inhabitants of their <a href="/wiki/Viking_expansion" title="Viking expansion">overseas settlements</a> during about the 9th to 13th centuries.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Proto-Norse_language" title="Proto-Norse language">Proto-Norse language</a> developed into Old Norse by the 8th century, and Old Norse began to develop into the modern North Germanic languages in the mid- to late 14th century, ending the language phase known as Old Norse. These dates, however, are not absolute, since written Old Norse is found well into the 15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Old Norse was divided into three <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialects</a>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_West_Norse" title="Old West Norse">Old West Norse</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_East_Norse" title="Old East Norse">Old East Norse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Old_Gutnish" title="Old Gutnish">Old Gutnish</a>. Old West and East Norse formed a <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continuum</a>, with no clear geographical boundary between them. For example, Old East Norse traits were found in eastern <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, although Old Norwegian is classified as Old West Norse, and Old West Norse traits were found in western <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>. Most speakers spoke Old East Norse in what is present day <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> and Sweden. Old Gutnish, the more obscure dialectal branch, is sometimes included in the Old East Norse dialect due to geographical associations. It developed its own unique features and shared in changes to both other branches.</p>
<p>The 12th-century Icelandic <i><a href="/wiki/Gray_Goose_Laws" title="Gray Goose Laws">Gray Goose Laws</a></i> state that Swedes, Norwegians, Icelanders and Danes spoke the same language, <i>dǫnsk tunga</i> ("Danish tongue"; speakers of Old East Norse would have said <i>dansk tunga</i>). Another term used, used especially commonly with reference to West Norse, was <i>norrœnt mál</i> ("Nordic speech"). Today Old Norse has developed into the modern North Germanic languages <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a>, of which Norwegian, Danish and Swedish retain considerable <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutual intelligibility</a>.</p>
<p>In some instances the term <i>Old Norse</i> refers specifically to Old West Norse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical_distribution">Geographical distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geographical distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Old_norse,_ca_900.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="909" data-file-width="1235" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG/250px-Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG/375px-Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG/500px-Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Old_norse,_ca_900.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The approximate extent of Old Norse and related languages in the early 10th century:
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <b>Old West Norse dialect</b></div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff9933; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <b>Old East Norse dialect</b></div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff00ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <b><a href="/wiki/Old_Gutnish" title="Old Gutnish">Old Gutnish</a></b></div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffff00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <b><a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a></b></div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #0000ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <b><a href="/wiki/Crimean_Gothic" title="Crimean Gothic">Crimean Gothic</a></b></div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00ff00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Other <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a> with which Old Norse still retained some mutual intelligibility</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Old_Norse#Old_Icelandic" title="Old Norse">Old Icelandic</a> was very close to <a href="/wiki/Old_Norwegian" title="Old Norwegian">Old Norwegian</a>, and together they formed the Old West Norse dialect, which was also spoken in settlements in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> and northwest England, and in Norse settlements in <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JohnsonEcyc_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-JohnsonEcyc-4">[4]</a></sup> The Old East Norse dialect was spoken in Denmark, Sweden, settlements in <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nationalencyklopedin_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nationalencyklopedin-5">[5]</a></sup> eastern England, and Danish settlements in <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Old_Gutnish" title="Old Gutnish">Old Gutnish</a> dialect was spoken in <a href="/wiki/Gotland" title="Gotland">Gotland</a> and in various settlements in the East. In the 11th century, Old Norse was the most widely spoken European language, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Vinland" title="Vinland">Vinland</a> in the West to the <a href="/wiki/Volga_River" title="Volga River">Volga River</a> in the East. In <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a>, it survived the longest in <a href="/wiki/Veliky_Novgorod" title="Veliky Novgorod">Veliky Novgorod</a>, probably lasting into the <a href="/wiki/13th_century" title="13th century">13th century</a> there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nationalencyklopedin_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nationalencyklopedin-5">[5]</a></sup> The age of the <a href="/wiki/Swedish-speaking_population_of_Finland" title="Swedish-speaking population of Finland">Swedish-speaking population of Finland</a> is strongly contested, but at latest by the time of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Swedish_Crusade" title="Second Swedish Crusade">Second Swedish Crusade</a> in the 13th century, Swedish settlement had spread the language into the region.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_descendants">Modern descendants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Modern descendants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">North Germanic languages</a></div>
<p>The modern descendants of the Old West Norse dialect are the West Scandinavian languages of <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a> and the extinct <a href="/wiki/Norn_language" title="Norn language">Norn language</a> of <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a>; the descendants of the Old East Norse dialect are the East Scandinavian languages of <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a>. Norwegian is descended from Old West Norse, but over the centuries it has been heavily influenced by East Norse, particularly during the <a href="/wiki/Denmark%E2%80%93Norway" title="Denmark–Norway">Denmark–Norway</a> union.</p>
<p>Among these, Icelandic and the closely related Faroese have changed the least from Old Norse in the last thousand years, although with Danish rule of the Faroe Islands, Faroese has also been influenced by Danish. Old Norse also had an influence on <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> dialects and <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Lowland Scots</a>, which contain many Old Norse <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a>. It also influenced the development of the <a href="/wiki/Norman_language" title="Norman language">Norman language</a>, and through it and to a smaller extent, that of modern <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>.</p>
<p>Various other languages, which are not closely related, have been heavily influenced by Norse, particularly the Norman dialects, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Primarily for the claim about Waterford Irish (June 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_language" title="Belarusian language">Belarusian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latvian_language" title="Latvian language">Latvian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a> also have a number of Norse loanwords; the words <i>Rus</i> and <i>Russia</i>, according to one theory, may be named after the <a href="/wiki/Rus%27_people" title="Rus' people">Rus' people</a>, a Norse tribe; <i>see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rus_(name)" title="Rus (name)">Rus (name)</a></i>, probably from present-day east-central Sweden. The current Finnish and Estonian words for Sweden are <i>Ruotsi</i> and <i>Rootsi</i>, respectively.</p>
<p>Of the modern languages, Icelandic is the closest to Old Norse. Written modern Icelandic derives from the Old Norse <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemic</a> writing system. Contemporary Icelandic-speakers can read Old Norse, which varies slightly in spelling as well as semantics and word order. However, pronunciation, particularly of the vowel phonemes, has changed at least as much as in the other North Germanic languages.</p>
<p>Faroese retains many similarities but is influenced by Danish, Norwegian, and <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">Gaelic</a> (<a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Although Swedish, Danish and the Norwegian languages have diverged the most, they still retain asymmetric <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutual intelligibility</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Speakers of modern Swedish, Norwegian and Danish can mostly understand each other without studying their neighboring languages, particularly if speaking slowly. The languages are also sufficiently similar in writing that they can mostly be understood across borders. This could be because these languages have been mutually affected by each other, as well as having a similar development influenced by <a href="/wiki/Middle_Low_German" title="Middle Low German">Middle Low German</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;width: 22em;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet"><img alt="[]" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="75" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/30px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/45px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/60px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 2x" width="30"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This section contains <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a> phonetic symbols.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet#Fonts" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">rendering support</a>, you may see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Replacement_character" title="Replacement character">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> characters.</td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Vowels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The vowel phonemes mostly come in pairs of long and short. The <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_orthography#Normalized_spelling" title="Old Norse orthography">standardized orthography</a> marks the long vowels with an acute accent. In medieval manuscripts, it is often unmarked but sometimes marked with an accent or through <a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">gemination</a>. All phonemes have, more or less, the expected phonetic realization.</p>
<p>Old Norse had nasalized versions of all nine vowel places.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CleasbyA_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-CleasbyA-9">[V 1]</a></sup> These occurred as allophones of the vowels before nasal consonants and in places where a nasal had followed it in an older form of the word, before it was absorbed into a neighboring sound. If the nasal was absorbed by a stressed vowel, it would also lengthen the vowel. These nasalizations also occurred in the other Germanic languages, but were not retained long. They were noted in the <a href="/wiki/First_Grammatical_Treatise" title="First Grammatical Treatise">First Grammatical Treatise</a>, and otherwise might have remained unknown. The First Grammarian marked these with a dot above the letter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CleasbyA_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-CleasbyA-9">[V 1]</a></sup> This notation did not catch on, and would soon be obsolete. Nasal and oral vowels probably merged around the 11th century in most of Old East Norse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NordicLanguages202_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-NordicLanguages202-10">[9]</a></sup><sup>:3</sup> However, the distinction still holds in <a href="/wiki/Dalecarlian_dialects" title="Dalecarlian dialects">Dalecarlian dialects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NordicLanguages202_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-NordicLanguages202-10">[9]</a></sup><sup>:4</sup> The dots in the following vowel table separate the oral from <a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">nasal</a> phonemes.</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Generic vowel system c. 9th–12th centuries</caption>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2"> </th>
<th colspan="4">Front vowels</th>

<th>High</th>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Close_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close front unrounded vowel">i</a></span> • <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ĩ</span></td>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">iː</span> • <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ĩː</span></td>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Close_front_rounded_vowel" title="Close front rounded vowel">y</a></span> • <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ỹ</span></td>

<th>Low/Low-mid</th>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">ɛ</a></span> • <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ɛ̃</span></td>

<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_rounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front rounded vowel">œ</a></span> • <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">œ̃</span></td>
<td> </td>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open front unrounded vowel">a</a></span> • <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ã</span></td>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">aː</span> • <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ãː</span></td>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Open-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open-mid back rounded vowel">ɔ</a></span> • <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ɔ̃</span></td>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Open-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open-mid back rounded vowel">ɔː</a></span> • <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ɔ̃ː</span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Note: The low/low-mid vowels may be indicated differently:</p>
<ul>
<li><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/æ/</span> = <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɛ/</span></li>
<li><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɒ/</span> = <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɔ/</span></li>
<li><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɑ/</span> = <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span></li>
</ul>
<p>Sometime around the 13th century, Ǫ (<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɔ/</span>) merged with Ø or O in all dialects except <a href="#Old_Danish">Old Danish</a>. In Icelandic, all Ǫ merged with Ø. This can be determined by their distinction within the 12th-century <a href="/wiki/First_Grammatical_Treatise" title="First Grammatical Treatise">First Grammatical Treatise</a> but not within the early 13th-century <a href="/wiki/Prose_Edda" title="Prose Edda">Prose Edda</a>. The nasal vowels, also noted in the First Grammatical Treatise, are assumed to have been lost by this time. See <a href="#Old_Icelandic">Old Icelandic</a> for the Œ &gt; Æ and Ę &gt; E mergers.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">Consonants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Consonants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Old Norse has six plosive phonemes. Of these <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/p/</span> is rare word-initially and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/d/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/b/</span> are realized as voiced fricative <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">allophones</a> between vowels, except in compound words (e.g. veðrabati), already in the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic language</a> (e.g. <i>*b</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*[β]</span> &gt; [v] between vowels). The <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɡ/</span> phoneme is realized as <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɡ]</span> after an <i>n</i> or another <i>g</i> and as <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[k]</span> before <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/s/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t/</span>. It is realized as a voiced velar fricative <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɣ]</span>, by some accounts inside words, and by others between vowels (and otherwise as <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɡ]</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweet_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sweet-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Scope of source in regards to /k/ before /s/, /t/; In general or in OIC? Date? (March 2010)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Old East Norse /ʀ/ was an <a href="/wiki/Apical_consonant" title="Apical consonant">apical consonant</a> whose position isn't precisely known, being reconstructed as a palatal <a href="/wiki/Sibilant" title="Sibilant">sibilant</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NordicLanguages202_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-NordicLanguages202-10">[9]</a></sup><sup>:2</sup>. It descended from Proto-Germanic <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/z/</span> and eventually developed into <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/r/</span>, as it already had done in Old West Norse.</p>

<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_and_alveolar_stops#Alveolar" title="Voiceless dental and alveolar stops">t</a></span> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental_and_alveolar_stops#Alveolar" title="Voiced dental and alveolar stops">d</a></span></td>

<td></td>
<td></td>

<p>The consonant digraphs <i>hl</i>, <i>hr</i>, <i>hn</i> occurred word-initially. It is unclear whether they were sequences of two consonants (with the first element realised as <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/h/</span> or perhaps <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/x/</span>), or as single voiceless sonorants <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l̥/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/r̥/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/n̥/</span> respectively. In Old Norwegian, Old Danish and later Old Swedish the groups <i>hl</i>, <i>hr</i>, <i>hn</i> were reduced to plain <i>l</i>, <i>r</i>, <i>n</i>, suggesting that they were most likely realised as voiceless sonorants by Old Norse times.</p>
<p>The pronunciation of <i>hv</i> is unclear, and may have been <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/xʷ/</span> (the Proto-Germanic pronunciation), <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/hʷ/</span> or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʍ/</span>. Unlike the other three groups above, it was retained much longer in all dialects, and never developed into a voiceless sonorant in Icelandic, but instead "<a href="/wiki/Fortition" title="Fortition">hardened</a>" to a plosive <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/kv/</span>. This suggests that it was not a voiceless sonorant, but retained stronger frication.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">Orthography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Orthography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_orthography" title="Old Norse orthography">Old Norse orthography</a></div>
<p>Unlike Proto-Norse, which was written with the <a href="/wiki/Elder_Futhark" title="Elder Futhark">Elder Futhark</a>, runic Old Norse was originally written with the <a href="/wiki/Younger_Futhark" title="Younger Futhark">Younger Futhark</a>, which only had 16 letters. Because of the limited number of runes, the rune for the vowel <i>u</i> was also used for the vowels <i>o</i>, <i>ø</i> and <i>y</i>, and the rune for <i>i</i> was used for <i>e</i>. <a href="/wiki/Medieval_runes" title="Medieval runes">Medieval runes</a> came into use some time later.</p>
<p>As for the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a>, there was no standardized orthography in use in the Middle Ages. A modified version of the letter <a href="/wiki/Wynn" title="Wynn">wynn</a> called <a href="/wiki/Vend_(letter)" title="Vend (letter)">vend</a> was used briefly for the sounds <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/v/</span>, and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/w/</span>. Long vowels were sometimes marked with acutes, but also sometimes left unmarked or geminated. The <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_orthography#Normalized_spelling" title="Old Norse orthography">standardized Old Norse spelling</a> was created in the 19th century, and is for the most part phonemic. The most notable deviation is that the non-phonemic difference between the <a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental_fricative" title="Voiced dental fricative">voiced</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_fricative" title="Voiceless dental fricative">voiceless dental fricative</a> is marked—the oldest texts as well as <a href="/wiki/Runic_inscriptions" title="Runic inscriptions">runic inscriptions</a> use <a href="/wiki/Thorn_(letter)" title="Thorn (letter)"><i>þ</i></a> exclusively. Long vowels are denoted with <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">acutes</a>. Most other letters are written with the same glyph as the <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a> phoneme, except as shown in the table below.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accent">Accent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Accent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/St%C3%B8d" title="Stød">Danish stød</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_phonology#Accent" title="Norwegian phonology">Norwegian tonal stress</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Swedish_phonology#Stress_and_pitch" title="Swedish phonology">Swedish tonal stress</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section needs expansion</b> with: Dating, etc.. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(April 2010)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Primary <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">stress</a> in Old Norse falls on the <a href="/wiki/Word_stem" title="Word stem">word stem</a>, so that <i>hyrjar</i> would be pronounced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ˈhyr.jar/</span>. In compound words, secondary stress falls on the second stem (e.g. <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/l%C3%A6risveinn#Old_Norse" title="wikt:lærisveinn">lærisveinn</a></i>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ˈlɛːɾ.iˌswɛinː/</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ProseReader_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-ProseReader-15">[14]</a></sup><sup>:1</sup></p>
<p>Modern Swedish, Danish, and Norwegian have two <a href="/wiki/Register_(phonology)" title="Register (phonology)">registers</a> reflected in differing pronunciation of the stressed syllable of words. In Swedish and Norwegian, the registers are reflected in different <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tones</a> (i.e. through <a href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" title="Pitch accent">tonal word accent</a>), while in Danish the difference is the presence or absence of <i>stød</i>, a glottal gesture considered a kind of <a href="/wiki/Creaky_voice" title="Creaky voice">creaky voice</a> that seems to have been documented by Swedish sources as early as the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> What is here called <i>class 1</i> is reflected as tone 1 in Norwegian, <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">acute accent</a> in Swedish, and presence of <i>stød</i> in Danish, whereas <i>class 2</i> words have tone 2 in Norwegian, <a href="/wiki/Grave_accent" title="Grave accent">grave accent</a> in Swedish, and no <i>stød</i> in Danish. No sign of any tonal system is found in Icelandic or Faroese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheNordicLanguages_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-TheNordicLanguages-17">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Not all cognates occur in the same register classes in all three languages, partly due to language-specific restrictions on the contexts in which the two classes can occur. For example, <i>stød</i> can only occur in stressed words that have long vowels or end in a voiced consonant, whereas in Swedish and Norwegian, monosyllables can only take tone 1/acute accent. In general, however, class 1 words are those that are monosyllabic in Old Norse, while class 2 words are those that are polysyllabic. Exceptions, including <a href="/wiki/Minimal_pair" title="Minimal pair">minimal pairs</a>, have arisen for various reasons:</p>
<ol>
<li>Suffixing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definite_article" title="Definite article">definite article</a> onto class 1 words does not change their class, suggesting that the register distinctions developed before the definite article fused with the noun. Hence Old Norse <i>and-inn</i> "the duck" &gt; Swedish class 1 <i>anden</i>, but <i>andi-nn</i> "the spirit" &gt; class 2 <i>anden</i>.</li>
<li>The Old Norse non-syllabic morpheme <i>-r</i> after a consonant later became syllabic through the <a href="/wiki/Epenthesis" title="Epenthesis">epenthesis</a> of a vowel, without changing the class. Hence Old Norse <i>bœndr</i> "farmers" &gt; Norwegian class 1 <i>bønder</i>, but <i>baunir</i> "beans" and <i>bœnir</i> "prayers" both &gt; Norwegian class 2 <i>bønner</i> (homophonous with <i>bønder</i> except for the pitch difference).</li>
<li>Many later polysyllabic loanwords in the various languages have acquired class 1.</li>
</ol>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonological_processes">Phonological processes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Phonological processes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ablaut">Ablaut</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ablaut">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Indo-European_ablaut" title="Indo-European ablaut">Ablaut</a></b> patterns are groups of vowels which are swapped, or <b>ablauted,</b> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syllable_nucleus" title="Syllable nucleus">nucleus</a> of a word. <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_morphology#Strong_verbs" title="Old Norse morphology">Strong verbs</a> ablaut the <a href="/wiki/Lemma_(morphology)" title="Lemma (morphology)">lemma</a>'s nucleus to derive the past forms of the verb. This parallels English conjugation, where, e.g., the nucleus of <i>sing</i> becomes <i>sang</i> in the past tense and <i>sung</i> in the past participle. Some verbs are derived by ablaut, as the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_morphology#Present-preterite" title="Old Norse morphology">present-in-past verbs</a> do by consequence of being derived from the past tense forms of strong verbs.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Umlaut">Umlaut</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Umlaut">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Germanic_umlaut" title="Germanic umlaut">Germanic umlaut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_morphology#Before_Old_Norse" title="Old Norse morphology">Old Norse morphophonology</a></div>
<p><b>Umlaut</b> or <b>mutation</b> is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assimilation_(linguistics)" title="Assimilation (linguistics)">assimilatory</a> process acting on vowels preceding a vowel or semivowel of a different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vowel_backness" title="Vowel backness">vowel backness</a>. In the case of <b>i-umlaut</b> and <b>ʀ-umlaut</b>, this entails a fronting of back vowels, with retention of lip rounding. In the case of <b>u-umlaut</b>, this entails <a href="/wiki/Labialization" title="Labialization">labialization</a> of unrounded vowels. Umlaut is phonemic and in many situations grammatically significant as a side effect of losing the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language#Morphology" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic morphological suffixes</a> whose vowels created the umlaut <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allophones" title="Allophones">allophones</a>.</p>
<p>Some <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/y/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/yː/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ø/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/øː/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɛ/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɛː/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/øy/</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FromOldNordic_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-FromOldNordic-11">[10]</a></sup> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɛi/</span> were obtained by <a href="/wiki/I-mutation" title="I-mutation">i-umlaut</a> from <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/uː/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/o/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/oː/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aː/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/au/</span>, and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ai/</span> respectively. Others were formed via ʀ-umlaut from <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/uː/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aː/</span>, and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/au/</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JohnsonEcyc_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-JohnsonEcyc-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/y/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/yː/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ø/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/øː/</span>, and all <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɔ/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɔː/</span> were obtained by u-umlaut from <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/iː/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/eː/</span>, and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aː/</span> respectively. See <a href="#Old_Icelandic">Old Icelandic</a> for information on <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɔː/</span>.</p>
<p><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/œ/</span> was obtained through a simultaneous u- and i-umlaut of <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span>. It appears in words like <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gera" title="wikt:gera">gøra</a> (gjǫra, geyra), from Proto-Germanic <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Appendix:Proto-Germanic/garwijanan" title="wikt:Appendix:Proto-Germanic/garwijanan">*garwijaną</a>, and commonly in verbs with a velar consonant before the suffix like <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/s%C3%B8kkva" title="wikt:søkkva">søkkva</a> &lt; <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Appendix:Proto-Germanic/sankwijanan" title="wikt:Appendix:Proto-Germanic/sankwijanan">*sankwijaną</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CleasbyOE_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-CleasbyOE-18">[V 2]</a></sup></p>
<p>OEN often preserves the original value of the vowel directly preceding runic <i>ʀ</i> while OWN receives ʀ-umlaut. Compare runic OEN <i>glaʀ, haʀi, hrauʀ</i> with OWN <i>gler, heri</i> (later <i>héri</i>), <i>hrøyrr/hreyrr</i> ("glass", "hare", "pile of rocks").</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="U-umlaut">U-umlaut</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: U-umlaut">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>U-umlaut is more common in Old West Norse in both phonemic and allophonic positions, while it only occurs sparsely in post-runic Old East Norse and even in runic Old East Norse. Compare West Old Norse <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fa%C3%B0ir#Old_Norse" title="wikt:faðir">fǫður</a></i> (accusative of <i>faðir</i>, father), <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/v%C7%ABr%C3%B0r#Old_Norse" title="wikt:vǫrðr">vǫrðr</a></i> (guardian/caretaker), <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%C7%ABrn#Old_Norse" title="wikt:ǫrn">ǫrn</a></i> (eagle), <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/j%C7%ABr%C3%B0#Old_Norse" title="wikt:jǫrð">jǫrð</a></i> (in Modern Icelandic: <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/j%C3%B6r%C3%B0#Icelandic" title="wikt:jörð">jörð</a></i>, earth), <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mj%C7%ABlk#Old_Norse" title="wikt:mjǫlk">mjǫlk</a></i> (in Modern Icelandic: <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mj%C3%B3lk#Icelandic" title="wikt:mjólk">mjólk</a></i>) with Old Swedish <i>faður</i>, <i>varðer</i>, <i>örn</i>, <i>jorð</i> and Modern Swedish <i>örn</i>, <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/jord" title="wikt:jord">jord</a></i>, <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mj%C3%B6lk" title="wikt:mjölk">mjölk</a></i> with the latter two demonstrating the u-umlaut found in Swedish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iversen_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iversen-20">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>This is still a major difference between Swedish and Faroese and Icelandic today. Plurals of neuters do not have u-umlaut at all in Swedish, but in Faroese and Icelandic they do, for example the Faroese and Icelandic plurals of the word <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/land" title="wikt:land">land</a></i>, <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/land#Faroese" title="wikt:land">lond</a></i> and <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/land#Icelandic" title="wikt:land">lönd</a></i> respectively, in contrast to the Swedish plural <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/land#Swedish" title="wikt:land">land</a></i> and numerous other examples. That also applies to almost all feminine nouns, for example the largest feminine noun group, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/O-stem" title="O-stem">o-stem</a> nouns (except the Swedish noun <i>jord</i> mentioned above), and even <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thematic_stem" title="Thematic stem">i-stem</a> nouns and <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_root#Word_formation" title="Proto-Indo-European root">root nouns</a>, such as Old West Norse <i>mǫrk</i> (<i><a class="external text" href="http://digicoll.library.wisc.edu/cgi-bin/IcelOnline/IcelOnline.TEId-idx?type=entry&amp;eid=MO3RK-1&amp;q1=mörk" rel="nofollow">mörk</a></i> in Icelandic) in comparison with Modern and Old Swedish <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mark#Swedish" title="wikt:mark">mark</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iversen_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Iversen-20">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breaking">Breaking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Breaking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Vowel_breaking" title="Vowel breaking">Vowel breaking</a></div>
<p><b>Vowel breaking</b>, or <b>fracture</b>, caused a front vowel to be split into a semivowel-vowel sequence before a back vowel in the following syllable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JohnsonEcyc_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-JohnsonEcyc-4">[4]</a></sup> While West Norse only broke <i>e</i>, East Norse also broke <i>i</i>. The change was blocked by a <i>v</i>, <i>l</i>, or <i>r</i> preceding the potentially-broken vowel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JohnsonEcyc_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-JohnsonEcyc-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NordicLanguages202_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-NordicLanguages202-10">[9]</a></sup><sup>:1</sup></p>
<p>Some <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ja/</span> or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/jɔ/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/jaː/</span> or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/jɔː/</span> result from breaking of <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/eː/</span> respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CleasbyPhonRules_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-CleasbyPhonRules-21">[V 3]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assimilation_or_elision_of_inflectional_.CA.80">Assimilation or elision of inflectional ʀ</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Assimilation or elision of inflectional ʀ">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When a noun, pronoun, adjective, or verb has a long vowel or diphthong in the accented syllable and its stem ends in a single -l, -n, or -s, the -r (or the elder r- or z-variant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yr_rune_(Younger_Futhark)" title="Yr rune (Younger Futhark)">ʀ</a>) in an ending is assimilated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CleasbyTableN_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-CleasbyTableN-22">[V 4]</a></sup> When the accented vowel is short, the ending is dropped.</p>
<p>The nominative of the strong masculine declension and some i-stem feminine nouns uses one such -r (ʀ). <i>Óðin</i>-<i>r</i> (<i>Óðin</i>-<i>ʀ</i>) becomes <i>Óðinn</i> instead of <i>*Óðinr</i> (<i>*Óðinʀ</i>), but <i>karl</i>-r (<i>karl</i>-<i>ʀ</i>) remains <i>karl</i>.</p>
<p><i>Blása</i>, to blow, has <i>blæss</i> for "you blow" instead of <i>*blæsr</i> (<i>*blæsʀ</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rule is not hard and fast, with counter-examples such as <i>vinr</i>, which has the synonym <i>vin</i>, yet retains the unabsorbed version, and <i>jǫtunn</i>, where assimilation takes place even though the root vowel, ǫ, is short.</p>
<p>Words with a final r in the <a href="/wiki/Word_stem" title="Word stem">word stem</a>, such as <i>vetr</i>, do not add another -r, as the sounds are already the same. The effect of the dropping usually results in the lack of distinction between some forms of the noun. In the case of <i>vetr</i> the dropping renders the nominative and accusative singular and plural identical; the nominative singular and nominative and accusative plural would otherwise have been <i>*vetrr</i> (<i>*vintrʀ</i>), while the accusative singular would still have been <i>vetr</i>. This is because the 3rd strong masculine declension, to which it belongs, marks the nominative singular and nominative and accusative plural, but not the accusative singular, with inflectional ʀs.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonotactics">Phonotactics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Phonotactics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blocking_of_ii.2C_uu">Blocking of ii, uu</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Blocking of ii, uu">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>I/j</i> adjacent to <i>i</i>, <i>e</i>, their u-umlauts, and <i>æ</i> was not possible, nor <i>u/v</i> adjacent to <i>u</i>, <i>o</i>, their i-umlauts, and <i>ǫ</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JohnsonEcyc_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-JohnsonEcyc-4">[4]</a></sup> At the beginning of words, this manifested as a dropping of the initial <i>j</i> or <i>v</i>. Compare ON <i>orð, úlfr, ár</i> with English <i>word, wolf, year</i>. In inflections, this manifested as the dropping of the inflectional vowels. Thus, <i>klæði</i> + <small>dat</small> <i>-i</i> remains <i>klæði</i>, and <i>sjáum</i> in Icelandic progressed to <i>sjǫ́um</i> &gt; <i>sjǫ́m</i> &gt; <i>sjám</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[20]</a></sup> The <i>jj</i> and <i>ww</i> of Proto-Germanic became <i>ggj</i> and <i>ggv</i> respectively in Old Norse, a change known as <a href="/wiki/Holtzmann%27s_law" title="Holtzmann's law">Holtzmann's law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JohnsonEcyc_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-JohnsonEcyc-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epenthesis">Epenthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Epenthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epenthetic" title="Epenthetic">epenthetic</a> vowel became popular by 1200 in Old Danish, 1250 in Old Swedish and Norwegian, and 1300 in Old Icelandic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheNordicLanguages_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-TheNordicLanguages-17">[16]</a></sup> An unstressed vowel was used which varied by dialect. Old Norwegian exhibited all three: /u/ was used in West Norwegian south of <a href="/wiki/Bergen" title="Bergen">Bergen</a>, as in <i>aftur</i>, <i>aftor</i> (older <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/aptr" title="wikt:aptr">aptr</a>); North of Bergen, /i/ appeared in <i>aftir</i>, <i>after</i>; and East Norwegian used /a/, <i>after</i>, <i>aftær</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FromOldNordic_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-FromOldNordic-11">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Old Norse was a moderately <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflected</a> language with high levels of nominal and verbal inflection. Most of the fused <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morphemes</a> are retained in modern Icelandic, especially in regard to noun case declensions, whereas modern Norwegian in comparison has moved towards more analytical word structures.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender">Gender</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Gender">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">Grammatical gender</a></div>
<p>Old Norse had three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">grammatical genders</a> – masculine, feminine and neuter. Adjectives or pronouns referring to a noun must <a href="/wiki/Agreement_(linguistics)" title="Agreement (linguistics)">mirror the gender of that noun</a>, so that one says, "heill maðr!" but, "heilt barn!" As in other languages, the grammatical gender of an impersonal noun is generally unrelated to an expected natural gender of that noun. While indeed <i>karl</i>, "man" is masculine, <i>kona</i>, "woman", is feminine, and <i>hús</i>, house, is neuter, so also are <i>hrafn</i> and <i>kráka</i>, for "raven" and "crow", masculine and feminine respectively, even in reference to a female raven or a male crow.</p>
<p>All neuter words have identical nominative and accusative forms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[21]</a></sup> and all feminine words have identical nominative and accusative plurals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The gender of some words' plurals does not agree with that of their singulars, such as <i>lim</i> and <i>mund</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[V 5]</a></sup> Some words, such as <i>hungr</i>, have multiple genders, evidenced by their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Determiner_(linguistics)" title="Determiner (linguistics)">determiners</a> being declined in different genders within a given sentence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hierarchy">Hierarchy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Hierarchy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Old Norse inherited the Proto-Germanic feature of having neuter as the default gender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[25]</a></sup> This means that when the gender of a noun is unknown, adjectives and pronouns referencing it use the neuter gender forms, rather than the masculine or feminine. Thus, if speaking or writing to a general audience, one would say <i>velkomit</i>, "well is it come," rather than <i>velkominn</i> or <i>velkomin</i>, "well is [he or she] come," as one does not know whether the person hearing it is going to be male or female.</p>
<p>One generally sees adjectives in their neuter form when used <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">pronominally</a> for this reason. For words more commonly used in this way (rather than to describe a noun) one sees their neuter forms more often than their masculine or feminine. Normally the masculine form would be the most beneficial form of an adjective to learn first, given that the majority of nouns are masculine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[26]</a></sup> In these cases, however, the most practical form to learn first would be the neuter.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology">Morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Morphology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_morphology" title="Old Norse morphology">Old Norse morphology</a></div>
<p>Nouns, adjectives and pronouns were <a href="/wiki/Declension" title="Declension">declined</a> in four grammatical cases—<a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative</a>, in singular and plural numbers. Adjectives and pronouns were additionally declined in three grammatical genders. Some pronouns (first and second person) could have <a href="/wiki/Dual_(grammatical_number)" title="Dual (grammatical number)">dual number</a> in addition to singular and plural. The genitive is used <a href="/wiki/Partitive_case" title="Partitive case">partitively</a>, and quite often in compounds and <a href="/wiki/Kenning" title="Kenning">kennings</a> (e.g.: <i><a href="/wiki/Ur%C3%B0arbrunnr" title="Urðarbrunnr">Urðarbrunnr</a></i>, the well of Urðr; <i><a href="/wiki/Lokasenna" title="Lokasenna">Lokasenna</a></i>, the gibing of Loki).</p>
<p>There were several classes of nouns within each gender, the following is an example of the "strong" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inflectional_paradigm" title="Inflectional paradigm">inflectional paradigms</a>:</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>

<td>trollum</td>
</tr>
