<p>In addition to these examples there were the numerous "weak" noun paradigms, which had a much higher degree of syncretism between the different cases in its paradigms, i.e. they had fewer forms than the "strong" nouns.</p>
<p>A definite article was realised as a suffix, that retained an independent declension e.g. <b>troll</b> (<i>a troll</i>) – <b>trollit</b> (<i>the troll</i>), <b>hǫll</b> ( <i>a hall</i>) – <b>hǫllin</b> (<i>the hall</i>), <b>armr</b> (<i>an arm</i>) – <b>armrinn</b> (<i>the arm</i>). This definite article, however, was a separate word, and did not become attached to the noun before later stages of the Old Norse period.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Texts">Texts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Texts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Commons-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1376" data-file-width="1024" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/30px-Commons-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/45px-Commons-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/59px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" width="30"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Wikimedia Commons has media related to <i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Old_Norse_manuscripts" title="commons:Category:Old Norse manuscripts">Old Norse manuscripts</a></b></i>.</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The earliest inscriptions in Old Norse are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Runic_alphabet" title="Runic alphabet">runic</a>, from the 8th century. Runes continued to be commonly used until the 15th century and have been recorded to be in use in some form as late as the 19th century in some parts of Sweden. With the conversion to Christianity in the 11th century came the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a>. The oldest preserved texts in Old Norse in the Latin alphabet date from the middle of the 12th century. Subsequently, Old Norse became the vehicle of a large and varied body of vernacular literature, unique in medieval Europe. Most of the surviving literature was written in Iceland. Best known are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norse_saga" title="Norse saga">Norse sagas</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Icelanders%27_sagas" title="Icelanders' sagas">Icelanders' sagas</a> and the mythological literature, but there also survives a large body of religious literature, translations into Old Norse of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Courtly_romance" title="Courtly romance">courtly romances</a>, classical mythology, and the Old Testament, as well as instructional material, <a href="/wiki/First_Grammatical_Treatise" title="First Grammatical Treatise">grammatical treatises</a> and a large body of letters and official documents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">Dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Dialects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Most of the innovations that appeared in Old Norse spread evenly through the Old Norse area. As a result, the dialects were very similar and considered to be the same language, a language that they sometimes called the Danish tongue (<i>Dǫnsk tunga</i>), sometimes Norse language (<i>Norrœnt mál</i>), as evidenced in the following two quotes from <a href="/wiki/Heimskringla" title="Heimskringla">Heimskringla</a> by <a href="/wiki/Snorri_Sturluson" title="Snorri Sturluson">Snorri Sturluson</a>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p><i>Móðir Dyggva var Drótt, dóttir Danps konungs, sonar Rígs er fyrstr var konungr kallaðr á danska tungu</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dyggvi" title="Dyggvi">Dyggvi</a>'s mother was <a href="/wiki/Druhtinaz" title="Druhtinaz">Drott</a>, the daughter of king Danp, <a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADgs%C3%BEula" title="Rígsþula">Ríg</a>'s son, who was the first to be called king in the Danish tongue.</p>
</blockquote>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p><i>...stirt var honum norrœnt mál, ok kylfdi mᴊǫk til orðanna, ok hǫfðu margir menn þat mᴊǫk at spotti</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>...the Norse language was hard for him, and he often fumbled for words, which amused people greatly.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>However, some changes were geographically limited and so created a dialectal difference between Old West Norse and Old East Norse.</p>
<p>As Proto-Norse evolved into Old Norse, in the 8th century, the effects of the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_umlaut" title="Germanic umlaut">umlauts</a> seem to have been very much the same over the whole Old Norse area. But in later dialects of the language a split occurred mainly between west and east as the use of umlauts began to vary. The typical umlauts (for example <i>fylla</i> from *<i>fullijan</i>) were better preserved in the West due to later generalizations in the east where many instances of umlaut were removed (many archaic Eastern texts as well as eastern runic inscriptions however portray the same extent of umlauts as in later Western Old Norse).</p>
<p>All the while, the changes resulting in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breaking_(linguistics)" title="Breaking (linguistics)">breaking</a> (for example <i>hiarta</i> from *<i>hertō</i>) were more influential in the East probably once again due to generalizations within the inflectional system. This difference was one of the greatest reasons behind the dialectalization that took place in the 9th and 10th centuries, shaping an Old West Norse dialect in Norway and the Atlantic settlements and an Old East Norse dialect in Denmark and Sweden.</p>
<p>Old West Norse and Old Gutnish did not take part in the monophthongization which changed <i>æi</i> (<i>ei</i>) into <i>ē</i>, <i>øy</i> (<i>ey</i>) and <i>au</i> into <i>ø̄</i>, nor did certain peripheral dialects of Swedish, as seen in modern <a href="/wiki/Swedish_dialects_in_Ostrobothnia" title="Swedish dialects in Ostrobothnia">Ostrobothnian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[30]</a></sup> Another difference was that Old West Norse lost certain combinations of consonants. The combinations -<i>mp</i>-, -<i>nt</i>-, and -<i>nk</i>- were assimilated into -<i>pp</i>-, -<i>tt</i>- and -<i>kk</i>- in Old West Norse, but this phenomenon was limited in Old East Norse.</p>
<p>Here is a comparison between the two dialects as well as Old Gutnish. It is a transcription from one of the <a href="/wiki/Funbo_Runestones" title="Funbo Runestones">Funbo Runestones</a> (U 990) meaning : <i>Veðr and Thane and Gunnar raised this stone after Haursi, their father. God help his spirit</i>:</p>
<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;">Veðr ok Þegn ok Gunnarr reistu stein þenna at Haursa, fǫður sinn. Guð hjalpi ǫnd hans. (OWN)</div>
<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;">Veðr ok Þegn ok Gunnarr ræistu stæin þenna at Haursa, faður sinn. Guð hialpi and hans (OEN)</div>
<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;">Veðr ok Þegn ok Gunnarr raistu stain þenna at Haursa, faður sinn. Guð hialpi and hans (OG)</div>
<p>The OEN original text above is transliterated according to traditional scholarly methods, wherein u-umlaut is not regarded in runic Old East Norse. Modern studies<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> have shown that the positions where it applies are the same as for runic Old West Norse. An alternative and probably more accurate transliteration would therefore render the text in OEN as such:</p>
<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;">Veðr ok Þegn ok Gunnarr ræistu stæin þenna at Haursa, fǫður sinn. Guð hialpi ǫnd hans (OEN)</div>
<p>Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Past_participle" title="Past participle">past participles</a> and other words underwent i-umlaut in Old West Norse but not in Old East Norse dialects. Examples of that are Icalandic slegið/sleginn and tekið/tekinn, which in Swedish are <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/slagit" title="wikt:slagit">slagit</a>/<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/slagen" title="wikt:slagen">slagen</a> and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/tagit" title="wikt:tagit">tagit</a>/<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/tagen" title="wikt:tagen">tagen</a>. This can also be seen in the Icelandic and Norwegian words <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sterkur" title="wikt:sterkur">sterkur</a> and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sterk" title="wikt:sterk">sterk</a> ("strong"), which in Swedish is <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/stark" title="wikt:stark">stark</a> as in Old Swedish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[31]</a></sup> These differences can also be seen in comparison between Norwegian and Swedish.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_West_Norse">Old West Norse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Old West Norse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The combinations -mp-, -nt-, and -nk- mostly merged to -pp-, -tt- and -kk- in Old West Norse at around the 7th century, marking the first distinction between the Eastern and Western dialects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NordicLanguages202_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-NordicLanguages202-10">[9]</a></sup><sup>:3</sup> The following table illustrates this:</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center">
<tr>
<th>English</th>
<th>Old West Norse</th>
<th>Old East Norse</th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Proto-Norse_language" title="Proto-Norse language">Proto-Norse</a></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>mushroom</td>
<td>s(v)ǫppr</td>
<td>svamper</td>
<td>*swampuz</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>steep</td>
<td>brattr</td>
<td>branter</td>
<td>*brantaz</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>widow</td>
<td>ekkja</td>
<td>ænkia</td>
<td>*ain(a)kjōn</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>to shrink</td>
<td><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/kreppa" title="wikt:kreppa">kreppa</a></td>
<td>krimpa</td>
<td>*krimpan</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>to sprint</td>
<td>spretta</td>
<td>sprinta</td>
<td>*sprintan</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>to sink</td>
<td>søkkva</td>
<td>sænkva</td>
<td>*sankwian</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>An early difference between Old West Norse and the other dialects was that Old West Norse had the forms <i>bú</i> "dwelling", <i>kú</i> "cow" (accusative) and <i>trú</i> "faith" whereas Old East Norse had <i>bó</i>, <i>kó</i> and <i>tró</i>. Old West Norse was also characterized by the preservation of <i>u</i>-umlaut, which meant that for example <a href="/wiki/Proto-Norse_language" title="Proto-Norse language">Proto-Norse</a> *<i>tanþu</i> "tooth" was pronounced <i>tǫnn</i> and not <i>tann</i> as in post-runic Old East Norse; OWN <i>gǫ́s</i> and runic OEN <i>gǫ́s</i>, while post-runic OEN <i>gás</i> "goose".</p>
<p>The earliest body of text appears in <a href="/wiki/Runic_inscriptions" title="Runic inscriptions">runic inscriptions</a> and in poems composed c. 900 by <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Ej%C3%B3%C3%B0%C3%B3lfr_of_Hvinir" title="Þjóðólfr of Hvinir">Þjóðólfr of Hvinir</a> (although the poems are not preserved in contemporary sources, but only in much later manuscripts). The earliest manuscripts are from the period 1150–1200 and concern both legal, religious and historical matters. During the 12th and 13th centuries, <a href="/wiki/Tr%C3%B8ndelag" title="Trøndelag">Trøndelag</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Norway" title="Western Norway">Western Norway</a> were the most important areas of the Norwegian kingdom and they shaped Old West Norse as an archaic language with a rich set of declensions. In the body of text that has come down to us from until c. 1300, Old West Norse had little dialect variation, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Icelandic" title="Old Icelandic">Old Icelandic</a> does not diverge much more than the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norwegian" title="Old Norwegian">Old Norwegian</a> dialects do from each other.</p>
<p>Old Norwegian differentiated early from Old Icelandic by the loss of the consonant <i>h</i> in initial position before <i>l</i>, <i>n</i> and <i>r</i>, thus whereas Old Icelandic manuscripts might use the form <i>hnefi</i> "fist", Old Norwegian manuscripts might use <i>nefi</i>.</p>
<p>From the late 13th century, Old Icelandic and Old Norwegian started to diverge more. After c. 1350, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> and following social upheavals seem to have accelerated language changes in Norway. From the late 14th century, the language used in Norway is generally referred to as <a href="/wiki/Old_Norwegian#Middle_Norwegian" title="Old Norwegian">Middle Norwegian</a>.</p>
<p>Old West Norse underwent a lengthening of initial vowels at some point, especially in Norwegian, so that OWN <i>eta</i> became <i>éta,</i> ONW <i>akr</i> &gt; <i>ákr</i>, OIC <i>ek</i> &gt; <i>ék</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Icelandic">Old Icelandic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Old Icelandic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Iceland, initial <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/w/</span> before <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɾ/</span> was lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[V 6]</a></sup> Compare Icelandic <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/rangur#Icelandic" title="wikt:rangur">rangur</a></i> with Norwegian <i>vrangr</i>, OEN <i>vrangʀ</i>. This change is shared with Old Gutnish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheNordicLanguages_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-TheNordicLanguages-17">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>A specifically Icelandic sound, the long, u-umlauted A, spelled Ǫ́ and pronounced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɔː/</span>, developed circa the early 11th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CleasbyA_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-CleasbyA-9">[V 1]</a></sup> It was short-lived, being marked in the <a href="/wiki/First_Grammatical_Treatise" title="First Grammatical Treatise">Grammatical Treatises</a> and remaining until the end of the 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CleasbyA_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-CleasbyA-9">[V 1]</a></sup></p>
<p><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/w/</span> merged with <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/v/</span> during the 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JohnsonEcyc_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-JohnsonEcyc-4">[4]</a></sup> This caused <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/v/</span> to become an independent phoneme from <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/f/</span>, and the written distinction of ⟨<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">v</span>⟩ for <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/v/</span> from medial and final ⟨<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">f</span>⟩ to become merely etymological.</p>
<p>Around the 13th century, Œ/Ǿ (<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/øː/</span>) merged to Æ (<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɛː/</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CleasbyAE_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-CleasbyAE-39">[V 7]</a></sup> Thus, pre-13th-century <i>grœnn</i> ‘green’ became modern Icelandic <i>grænn</i>. The 12th-century <a href="/wiki/Gray_Goose_Laws" title="Gray Goose Laws">Grágás</a> manuscripts distinguish the vowels, and so the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Regius" title="Codex Regius">Codex Regius</a> copy does as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CleasbyAE_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-CleasbyAE-39">[V 7]</a></sup> However, the 13th-century Codex Regius copy of the <a href="/wiki/Poetic_Edda" title="Poetic Edda">Poetic Edda</a> probably relied on newer and/or poorer quality sources—demonstrating either difficulty with or total lack of natural distinction, the manuscripts show separation of the two phonemes in some places, but frequently mix up the letters chosen to distinguish them in others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CleasbyAE_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-CleasbyAE-39">[V 7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Towards the end of the 13th century, Ę (<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɛ/</span>) merged to E (<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e/</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CleasbyE_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-CleasbyE-41">[V 8]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Norwegian">Old Norwegian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Old Norwegian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Old_Norwegian" title="Old Norwegian">Old Norwegian</a></div>
<p>Around the 11th century,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Old Norwegian ⟨<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">hl</span>⟩, ⟨<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">hn</span>⟩, and ⟨<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">hr</span>⟩ became ⟨<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">l</span>⟩, ⟨<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">n</span>⟩, and ⟨<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">r</span>⟩. It is debatable whether the ⟨<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">hC</span>⟩ sequences represented a consonant cluster, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/hC/</span>, or a devoicing, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/C̥/</span>.</p>
<p>Orthographic evidence suggests that, in a confined dialect of Old Norwegian, /ɔ/ may have been unrounded before /u/, so that u-umlaut was reversed where the <i>u</i> had not been eliminated. e.g. <i>ǫll</i>, <i>ǫllum</i> &gt; <i>ǫll</i>, <i>allum</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenlandic_Norse">Greenlandic Norse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Greenlandic Norse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Greenlandic_Norse" title="Greenlandic Norse">Greenlandic Norse</a></div>
<p>This dialect of Old West Norse was spoken by Icelandic colonies in Greenland. When the colonies died out around the 15th century, the dialect went with it. The phoneme <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/θ/</span>, and some <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ð/</span> merged to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t/</span>, so that Old Icelandic <b>Þórðr</b> becomes <b>Tortr</b>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Text_example">Text example</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Text example">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_orthography" title="Old Norse orthography">Old Norse orthography</a></div>
<p>The following text is from <i>Alexanders saga</i>, an <a href="/wiki/Alexander_romance" title="Alexander romance">Alexander romance</a>. The manuscript, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=AM_519_a_4to&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="AM 519 a 4to (page does not exist)">AM 519 a 4to</a>, is dated c. 1280. The facsimile demonstrates the <a href="/wiki/Scribal_abbreviation" title="Scribal abbreviation">sigla</a> used by scribes to write Old Norse. Many of these were borrowed from Latin. Without familiarity with these abbreviations, the facsimile will be unreadable to many. In addition, reading the manuscript itself requires familiarity with the letterforms of the native script. The abbreviations are expanded in a version with normalized spelling like the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_orthography" title="Old Norse orthography">standard normalization</a> system's. Comparing this to the spelling of the same text in Modern Icelandic shows that, while pronunciation has changed greatly, spelling has changed little.</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th>Digital facsimile of the manuscript text<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeNoTa14_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-MeNoTa14-43">[35]</a></sup></th>
<th>The same text with normalized spelling<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeNoTa14_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-MeNoTa14-43">[35]</a></sup></th>
<th>The same text in Modern <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>
<p>[...] ſem oꝩın͛ h̅ſ brıgzloðo h̅o̅ epꞇ͛ þͥ ſe̅ ſıðaʀ mon ſagꞇ verða. Þeſſı ſveın̅ aͬ.* ꝩar ıſcola ſeꞇꞇr ſem ſıðꝩenıa e͛ ꞇıl rıkra man̅a vꞇan-lanꝺz aꞇ laꞇa g͛a vıð boꝛn̅ ſíıƞ́ Meıſꞇarı ꝩar h̅o̅ ꝼengın̅ ſa e͛ arıſꞇoꞇıleſ heꞇ. h̅ ꝩar harðla goðꝛ clercr ⁊ <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_morphology#Hit" title="Old Norse morphology">en̅</a> meſꞇı ſpekıngr aꞇ ꝩıꞇı. ⁊ er h̅ ꝩͬ .xíí. veꞇᷓ gamall aꞇ allꝺrı nalıga alroſcın̅ aꞇ ꝩıꞇı. en ſꞇoꝛhvgaðꝛ u̅ ꝼᷓm alla ſına ıaꝼnallꝺꝛa.</p>
</td>
<td>
<p>[...] sem óvinir hans brigzluðu honum eftir því, sem síðarr man sagt verða. þessi sveinn Alexander var í skóla settr, sem siðvenja er til ríkra manna útanlands at láta gera við bǫrn sín. meistari var honum fenginn sá, er Aristoteles hét. hann var harðla góðr klerkr ok inn mesti spekingr at viti. ok er hann var 12 vetra gamall at aldri, náliga alroskinn at viti, en stórhugaðr umfram alla sína jafnaldra, [...]</p>
</td>
<td>
<p>[...] sem óvinir hans brigsluðu honum eftir því, sem síðar mun sagt verða. Þessi sveinn Alexander var í skóla settur, sem siðvenja er til ríkra manna utanlands að láta gera við börn sín. Meistari var honum fenginn sá, er Aristóteles hét. Hann var harðla góður klerkur og hinn mesti spekingur að viti og er hann var 12 vetra gamall að aldri, nálega alroskinn að viti en stórhugaður umfram alla sína jafnaldra [...]</p>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>* <b>a</b> printed in <a href="/wiki/Uncial_script" title="Uncial script">uncial</a>. Uncials not encoded separately in Unicode as of this section's writing.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_East_Norse">Old East Norse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Old East Norse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:R%C3%B6kstenen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="904" data-file-width="515" height="439" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/R%C3%B6kstenen.jpg/250px-R%C3%B6kstenen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/R%C3%B6kstenen.jpg/375px-R%C3%B6kstenen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/R%C3%B6kstenen.jpg/500px-R%C3%B6kstenen.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:R%C3%B6kstenen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B6k_Runestone" title="Rök Runestone">Rök Runestone</a> in <a href="/wiki/%C3%96sterg%C3%B6tland" title="Östergötland">Östergötland</a>, Sweden, is the longest surviving source of early Old East Norse. It is inscribed on both sides.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Old East Norse, between 800 and 1100, is in Sweden called <i>Runic Swedish</i> and in Denmark <i>Runic Danish</i>. The use of <i>Swedish</i> and <i>Danish</i> is not for linguistic reasons as the differences between them are minute at best during the more ancient stages of this dialect group. Changes had a tendency to occur earlier in the Danish region and until this day many Old Danish changes have still not taken place in modern Swedish rendering Swedish as the more archaic out of the two concerning both the ancient and the modern languages, sometimes by a profound margin but in all differences are still minute. They are called <i>runic</i> because the body of text appears in <a href="/wiki/Runes" title="Runes">runes</a>.</p>
<p>Runic Old East Norse is characteristically archaic in form, especially Swedish (which is still true for modern Swedish compared to Danish). In essence it matches or surpasses the archaicness of post-runic Old West Norse which in its turn is generally more archaic than post-runic Old East Norse. While typically "Eastern" in structure, many later post-runic changes and trademarks of EON had yet to happen.</p>
<p>The phoneme <i>ʀ</i>, which evolved during the Proto-Norse period from <i>z</i>, was still clearly separated from <i>r</i> in most positions, even when being geminated, while in OWN it had already merged with <i>r</i>.</p>
<p>Monophthongization of <i>æi &gt; ē</i> and <i>øy, au &gt; ø̄</i> started in mid-10th-century Denmark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FromOldNordic_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-FromOldNordic-11">[10]</a></sup> Compare runic OEN: <i>fæigʀ</i>, <i>gæiʀʀ</i>, <i>haugʀ</i>, <i>møydōmʀ</i>, <i>diūʀ</i>; with Post-runic OEN: <i>fēgher</i>, <i>gēr</i>, <i>hø̄gher</i>, <i>mø̄dōmber</i>, <i>diūr</i>; OWN: <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/feigr" title="wikt:feigr">feigr</a></i>, <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/geirr" title="wikt:geirr">geirr</a></i>, <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/howe" title="wikt:howe">haugr</a></i>, <i>meydómr</i>, <i>dýr</i>; from PN *faigiaz, *gaizaz, *haugaz, *mawi- + dōmaz (maidendom; virginity), *diuza ((wild) animal).</p>
<p>Feminine o-stems often preserve the plural ending -aʀ while in OWN they more often merge with the feminine i-stems: (runic OEN) <i>*sōlaʀ</i>, <i>*hafnaʀ</i>/<i>*hamnaʀ</i>, <i>*vāgaʀ</i> while OWN <i>sólir</i>, <i>hafnir</i> and <i>vágir</i> (modern Swedish <i>solar</i>, <i>hamnar</i>, <i>vågar</i>; suns, havens, scales; Danish has mainly lost the distinction between the two stems with both endings now being rendered as -er or -e alternatively for the o-stems).</p>
<p>Vice versa, masculine i-stems with the root ending in either <i>g</i> or <i>k</i> tended to shift the plural ending to that of the ja-stems while OEN kept the original: <i>drængiaʀ</i>, <i>*ælgiaʀ</i> and <i>*bænkiaʀ</i> while OWN <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/drengr#Old_Norse" title="wikt:drengr">drengir</a></i>, <i>elgir</i> (elks) and <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bekkr#Etymology_1" title="wikt:bekkr">bekkir</a></i> (modern Danish "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/dreng" title="wikt:dreng">drenge</a>", "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/elg" title="wikt:elg">elge</a>", "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/b%C3%A6nk" title="wikt:bænk">bænke</a>", modern Swedish <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/dr%C3%A4ng" title="wikt:dräng">drängar</a></i>, <i>älgar</i>, <i>bänkar</i>).</p>
<p>The plural ending of ja-stems were mostly preserved while those of OEN often acquired that of the i-stems: <i>*bæðiaʀ</i>, <i>*bækkiaʀ</i>, <i>*væfiaʀ</i> while OWN <i>beðir</i> (beds), <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bekkr#Etymology_2" title="wikt:bekkr">bekkir</a></i>, <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/vefr#Old_Norse" title="wikt:vefr">vefir</a></i> (modern Swedish <i>bäddar</i>, <i>bäckar</i>, <i>vävar</i>).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Danish">Old Danish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Old Danish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Danish" title="History of Danish">History of Danish</a></div>
<p>Until the early 12th century, Old East Norse was very much a uniform dialect. It was in Denmark that the first innovations appeared that would differentiate Old Danish from Old Swedish<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NordicLanguages202_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-NordicLanguages202-10">[9]</a></sup><sup>:3</sup> as these innovations spread north unevenly (unlike the earlier changes that spread more evenly over the East Norse area) creating a series of <a href="/wiki/Isogloss" title="Isogloss">isoglosses</a> going from <a href="/wiki/Zealand" title="Zealand">Zealand</a> to <a href="/wiki/Svealand" title="Svealand">Svealand</a>.</p>
<p>In Old Danish, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/hɾ/</span> merged with <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɾ/</span> during the 9th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[36]</a></sup> From the 11th to 14th centuries, the unstressed vowels -<i>a</i>, -<i>o</i> and -<i>e</i> (<a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_orthography#Normalized_spelling" title="Old Norse orthography">standard normalization</a> -<i>a</i>, -<i>u</i> and -<i>i</i>) started to merge into -<i>ə</i>, represented with the letter <i>e</i>. This vowel came to be <a href="/wiki/Epenthesis" title="Epenthesis">epenthetic</a>, particularly before <i>-ʀ</i> endings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheNordicLanguages_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-TheNordicLanguages-17">[16]</a></sup> At the same time, the voiceless <a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">stop consonants</a> <i>p</i>, <i>t</i> and <i>k</i> became voiced plosives and even <a href="/wiki/Fricative_consonant" title="Fricative consonant">fricative consonants</a>. Resulting from these innovations, Danish has <i>kage</i> (cake), <i>tunger</i> (tongues) and <i>gæster</i> (guests) whereas (Standard) Swedish has retained older forms, <i>kaka</i>, <i>tungor</i> and <i>gäster</i> (OEN <i>kaka</i>, <i>tungur</i>, <i>gæstir</i>).</p>
<p>Moreover, the Danish <a href="/wiki/Pitch_accent#Norwegian_and_Swedish" title="Pitch accent">pitch accent</a> shared with Norwegian and Swedish changed into <i><a href="/wiki/St%C3%B8d" title="Stød">stød</a></i> around this time.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Swedish">Old Swedish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Old Swedish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Old_Swedish" title="Old Swedish">Old Swedish</a></div>
<p>At the end of the 10th and early 11th century initial <i>h-</i> before <i>l</i>, <i>n</i> and <i>r</i> was still preserved in the middle and northern parts of Sweden, and is sporadically still preserved in some northern dialects as <i>g-</i>, e.g. <i>gly</i> (lukewarm), from <i>hlýʀ</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Dalecarlian_dialects" title="Dalecarlian dialects">Dalecarlian dialects</a> developed as Old Swedish dialects and as such can be considered separate languages from Swedish.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Text_example_2">Text example</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Text example">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>This is an extract from <i><a href="/wiki/V%C3%A4stg%C3%B6talagen" title="Västgötalagen">Västgötalagen</a></i>, the Westrogothic law. It is the oldest text written as a manuscript found in Sweden and from the 13th century. It is contemporaneous with most of the Icelandic literature. The text marks the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Old_Swedish" title="Old Swedish">Old Swedish</a> as a distinct dialect.</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Dræpær maþar svænskan man eller smalenskæn, innan konongsrikis man, eigh væstgøskan, bøte firi atta ørtogher ok þrettan markær ok ænga ætar bot. [...] Dræpar maþær danskan man allæ noræn man, bøte niv markum. Dræpær maþær vtlænskan man, eigh ma frid flyia or landi sinu oc j æth hans. Dræpær maþær vtlænskæn prest, bøte sva mykit firi sum hærlænskan man. Præstær skal i bondalaghum væræ. Varþær suþærman dræpin ællær ænskær maþær, ta skal bøta firi marchum fiurum þem sakinæ søkir, ok tvar marchar konongi.</p>
<p>If someone slays a <a href="/wiki/Swedes_(Germanic_tribe)" title="Swedes (Germanic tribe)">Swede</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Sm%C3%A5land" title="Småland">Smålander</a>, a man from the kingdom, but not a West <a href="/wiki/Geats" title="Geats">Geat</a>, he will pay eight <a href="/wiki/%C3%96rtug" title="Örtug">örtugar</a> (20-pence coins) and thirteen marks, but no <a href="/wiki/Weregild" title="Weregild">weregild</a>. [...] If someone slays a Dane or a Norwegian, he will pay nine marks. If someone slays a foreigner, he shall not be banished and have to flee to his <a href="/wiki/Norse_clans" title="Norse clans">clan</a>. If someone slays a foreign priest, he will pay as much as for a fellow countryman. A priest counts as a freeman. If a Southerner is slain or an Englishman, he shall pay four marks to the plaintiff and two marks to the king.</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Gutnish">Old Gutnish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Old Gutnish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_Gutnish" title="Old Gutnish">Old Gutnish</a></div>
<p>Due to <a href="/wiki/Gotland" title="Gotland">Gotland</a>'s early isolation from the mainland, many features of Old Norse did not spread from or to the island, and Old Gutnish developed as an entirely separate branch from Old East and West Norse. For example, the diphthong <i>ai</i> in <i>aigu</i>, <i>þair</i> and <i>waita</i> was not retroactively umlauted to <i>ei</i> as in e.g. Old Icelandic <i>eigu</i>, <i>þeir</i> and <i>veita</i>. Breaking was especially active in Old Gutnish, leading to forms such as <i>bjera</i> and <i>bjauþa</i>, mainland <i>bera</i> and <i>bjúþa</i>. Dropping of /w/ in initial /wɾ/ is shared only with Old Icelandic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheNordicLanguages_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-TheNordicLanguages-17">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Text_example_3">Text example</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Text example">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Gutasaga" title="Gutasaga">Gutasaga</a> is the longest text surviving from <a href="/wiki/Old_Gutnish" title="Old Gutnish">Old Gutnish</a>. It was written in the 13th century and dealt with the early history of the Gotlanders. This part relates to the agreement that the Gotlanders had with the Swedish king sometime before the 9th century:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>So gingu gutar sielfs wiliandi vndir suia kunung þy at þair mattin frir Oc frelsir sykia suiariki j huerium staþ. vtan tull oc allar utgiftir. So aigu oc suiar sykia gutland firir vtan cornband ellar annur forbuþ. hegnan oc hielp sculdi kunungur gutum at waita. En þair wiþr þorftin. oc kallaþin. sendimen al oc kunungr oc ierl samulaiþ a gutnal þing senda. Oc latta þar taka scatt sinn. þair sendibuþar aighu friþ lysa gutum alla steþi til sykia yfir haf sum upsala kunungi til hoyrir. Oc so þair sum þan wegin aigu hinget sykia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>So, by their own will, the <a href="/wiki/Gotlander" title="Gotlander">Gotlanders</a> became the subjects of the Swedish king, so that they could travel freely and without risk to any location in the Swedish kingdom without toll and other fees. Likewise, the Swedes had the right to go to Gotland without corn restrictions or other prohibitions. The king was to provide protection and help, when they needed it and asked for it. The king and the <a href="/wiki/Earl" title="Earl">jarl</a> shall send emissaries to the Gutnish <a href="/wiki/Thing_(assembly)" title="Thing (assembly)">thing</a> to receive the taxes. These emissaries shall declare free passage for the Gotlanders to all locations in the sea of the king at <a href="/wiki/Gamla_Uppsala" title="Gamla Uppsala">Uppsala</a> (that is the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> was under Swedish control) and likewise for everyone who wanted to travel to Gotland.</p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_to_other_languages">Relationship to other languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Relationship to other languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_to_English">Relationship to English</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Relationship to English">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_English#Scandinavian_influence" title="History of English">History of English § Scandinavian influence</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_words_of_Old_Norse_origin" title="List of English words of Old Norse origin">List of English words of Old Norse origin</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> and Old Norse were related languages. It is therefore not surprising that many words in Old Norse look familiar to English speakers (e.g., <i>armr</i> (arm), <i>fótr</i> (foot), <i>land</i> (land), <i>fullr</i> (full), <i>hanga</i> (to hang), <i>standa</i> (to stand)). This is because both <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> and Old Norse stem from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a> mother language. In addition, numerous common, everyday Old Norse words were adopted into the Old English language during the Viking age. A few examples of Old Norse <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a> in modern English are (English/Viking age Old East Norse), in some cases even displacing their Old English cognates:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Nouns</b> – <i>anger</i> (angr), <i>bag</i> (baggi), <i>bait</i> (bæit, bæita, bæiti), <i>band</i> (band), <i>bark</i> (bǫrkʀ, stem bark-), <i>birth</i> (byrðr), <i>dirt</i> (drit), <i>dregs</i> (dræggiaʀ), <i>egg</i> (ægg, related to OE. cognate "æg" which became Middle English "eye"/"eai"), <i>fellow</i> (félagi), <i>gap</i> (gap), <i>husband</i> (húsbóndi), <i>cake</i> (kaka), <i>keel</i> (kiǫlʀ, stem also kial-, kil-), <i>kid</i> (kið), <i>knife</i> (knífʀ), <i>law</i> (lǫg, stem lag-), <i>leg</i> (læggʀ), <i>link</i> (hlænkʀ), <i>loan</i> (lán, related to OE. cognate "læn", cf. lend), <i>race</i> (rǫs, stem rás-), <i>root</i> (rót, related to OE. cognate "wyrt", cf. <a href="/wiki/List_of_wort_plants" title="List of wort plants">wort</a>), <i>sale</i> (sala), <i>scrap</i> (skrap), <i>seat</i> (sæti), <i>sister</i> (systir, related to OE. cognate "sweostor"), <i>skill</i> (skial/skil), <i>skin</i> (skinn), <i>skirt</i> (skyrta vs. the native English <i>shirt</i> of the same root), <i>sky</i> (ský), <i>slaughter</i> (slátr), <i>snare</i> (snara), <i>steak</i> (stæik), <i>thrift</i> (þrift), <i>tidings</i> (tíðindi), <i>trust</i> (traust), <i>window</i> (vindauga), <i>wing</i> (væ(i)ngʀ)</li>
<li><b>Verbs</b> – <i>are</i> (er, displacing OE "sind") <i>blend</i> (blanda), <i>call</i> (kalla), <i>cast</i> (kasta), <i>clip</i> (klippa), <i>crawl</i> (krafla), <i>cut</i> (possibly from ON kuta), <i>die</i> (døyia), <i>gasp</i> (gæispa), <i>get</i> (geta), <i>give</i> (gifa/gefa, related to OE. cognate "giefan"), <i>glitter</i> (glitra), <i>hit</i> (hitta), <i>lift</i> (lyfta), <i>raise</i> (ræisa), <i>ransack</i> (rannsaka), <i>rid</i> (ryðia), <i>run</i> (rinna, stem rinn-/rann-/runn-, related to OE. cognate "rinnan"), <i>scare</i> (skirra), <i>scrape</i> (skrapa), <i>seem</i> (søma), <i>sprint</i> (sprinta), <i>take</i> (taka), <i>thrive</i> (þrífa(s)), <i>thrust</i> (þrysta), <i>want</i> (vanta)</li>
<li><b>Adjectives</b> – <i>flat</i> (flatr), <i>happy</i> (happ), <i>ill</i> (illr), <i>likely</i> (líklígʀ), <i>loose</i> (lauss), <i>low</i> (lágʀ), <i>meek</i> (miúkʀ), <i>odd</i> (odda), <i>rotten</i> (rotinn/rutinn), <i>scant</i> (skamt), <i>sly</i> (sløgʀ), <i>weak</i> (væikʀ), <i>wrong</i> (vrangʀ)</li>
<li><b>Adverbs</b> – <i>thwart/athwart</i> (þvert)</li>
<li><b>Prepositions</b> – <i>till</i> (til), <i>fro</i> (frá)</li>
<li><b>Conjunction</b> – though/tho (þó)</li>
<li><b>Interjection</b> – <i>hail</i> (hæill), <i>wassail</i> (ves hæill)</li>
<li><b>Personal pronoun</b> – <i>they</i> (þæiʀ), <i>their</i> (þæiʀa), <i>them</i> (þæim) (for which the Anglo-Saxons said <i>híe</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[38]</a></sup> <i>hiera</i>, <i>him</i>)</li>
<li><b>Prenominal adjectives</b> – <i>same</i> (sami)</li>
</ul>
<p>In a simple sentence like "They are both weak" the extent of the Old Norse loanwords becomes quite clear (Old East Norse with archaic pronunciation: "Þæiʀ eʀu báðiʀ wæikiʀ" while Old English "híe syndon bégen (þá) wáce"). The words "they" and "weak" are both borrowed from Old Norse, and the word "both" might also be a borrowing, though this is disputed (cf. German <i>beide</i>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (December 2010)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> While the number of loanwords adopted from the Norse was not as numerous as that of Norman French or Latin, their depth and everyday nature make them a substantial and very important part of every day English speech as they are part of the very core of the modern English vocabulary.</p>
<p>Words like "bull" and "Thursday" are more difficult when it comes to their origins.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> "Bull" may be from either Old English "bula" or Old Norse "buli",<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> while "Thursday" may be a borrowing, or it could simply be from the Old English "Þunresdæg", which could have been influenced by the Old Norse cognate.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The word "are" is from Old English "earun"/"aron", which stems back to Proto-Germanic as well as the Old Norse cognates.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_to_modern_Scandinavian_languages">Relationship to modern Scandinavian languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Relationship to modern Scandinavian languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center;">
<caption>Development of Old Norse vowels to the modern Scandinavian languages</caption>
<tr>
<th>Old Norse</th>
<th>Modern<br/>
Icelandic</th>
<th>Modern<br/>
Faroese</th>
<th>Modern<br/>
Swedish<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-helfenstein_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-helfenstein-47">[39]</a></sup></th>
<th>Modern<br/>

<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">NB = <a href="/wiki/Bokm%C3%A5l" title="Bokmål">Bokmål</a> <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a>, a mixture of Danish and pure Norwegian; NN = <a href="/wiki/Nynorsk" title="Nynorsk">Nynorsk</a> <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a>, based on West Norwegian dialects and without Danish influence; No = same in both forms of Norwegian.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-vowel-length-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-vowel-length_49-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-vowel-length_49-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-vowel-length_49-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Vowel length in the modern Scandinavian languages does not stem from Old Norse vowel length. In all of the modern languages, Old Norse vowel length was lost, and vowel length became allophonically determined by syllable structure, with long vowels occurring when followed by zero or one consonants (and some clusters, e.g. in Icelandic, most clusters of obstruent to <a href="/wiki/Obstruent" title="Obstruent">obstruent</a> + [r], [j] or [v], such as [pr], [tj], [kv] etc.); short vowels occurred when followed by most consonant clusters, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_letter" title="Double letter">double consonants</a>. Often, pairs of short and long vowels became differentiated in quality before the loss of vowel length and thus did not end up merging; e.g. Old Norse /a aː i iː/ became Icelandic /a au ɪ i/, all of which can occur allophonically short or long. In the mainland Scandinavian languages, double consonants were reduced to single consonants, making the new vowel length phonemic.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-swedish-hook-o-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-swedish-hook-o_50-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-swedish-hook-o_50-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">⟨o⟩ or (before /r/) ⟨ø⟩ in some isolated words, but the tendency was to restore ⟨a⟩.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">When un-umlauted */u/ is still present elsewhere in the paradigm.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">When not followed by a consonant.</span></li>
</ol>

<li>Lass, Roger. <i><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=PhzIWORqgVgC#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">Old English: A Historical Linguistic Companion</a></i>. Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, (1993).</li>
</ul>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dictionaries">Dictionaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Norse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Dictionaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><i>An Icelandic–English Dictionary</i> (1874), <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cleasby" title="Richard Cleasby">Richard Cleasby</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gudbrand_Vigfusson" title="Gudbrand Vigfusson">Gudbrand Vigfusson</a>. @ <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/icelandicenglish00cleauoft#page/n0/mode/2up" rel="nofollow">Internet Archive</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://lexicon.ff.cuni.cz/texts/oi_cleasbyvigfusson_about.html" rel="nofollow">Germanic Lexicon Project</a> (HTML, PNG, TIFF)</li>
<li>G. T. Zoëga, <i>A Concise Dictionary of Old Icelandic</i> (1910),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[1]</a></sup> <a class="external text" href="http://lexicon.ff.cuni.cz/texts/oi_zoega_about.html" rel="nofollow">Univerzita Karlova – UK</a> <span class="languageicon" style="font-size:0.95em; font-weight:bold; color:#555;">(Czech)</span></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://onp.ku.dk/english/" rel="nofollow">"Ordbog over det norrøne prosasprog – A Dictionary of Old Norse Prose."</a> Copenhagen 1989–. (Scientific dictionary of Old Norse Prose texts. Digital version of printed volumes 1–3 (a-em), word-list, Indices and all the dictionary's slips/citations (en-ǫ) as well as newly edited material available on-line. <span class="languageicon" style="font-size:0.95em; font-weight:bold; color:#555;">(Danish)</span> and <span class="languageicon" style="font-size:0.95em; font-weight:bold; color:#555;">(English)</span>)</li>
<li>Jan de Vries, <i>Altnordisches Etymologisches Wörterbuch</i> (1977)</li>
<li>Finnur Jónsson, <i>Lexicon poeticum antiquæ linguæ septentrionalis</i> (1931): a dictionary of Old Norse poetry <a class="external autonumber" href="http://www.septentrionalia.net/etexts/" rel="nofollow">[2]</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[2]</a></sup></li>
